1	    
Abstract 
Bacillus cereus sensu lato is a ubiquitous pathogen, that can be isolated from several ready-to-eat 
(RTE) foods. RTE foods may cause food poisoning or food spoilage, if they are not sterilized by 
heat treatment before consumption. Bacillus cereus spores may be present in several types of 
cooked foods, and due to their ability to survive high cooking temperatures. Therefore, it is required 
that RTE foods are hold-hold at temperatures that inhibit the growth of these bacteria. In the present 
study, the growth ability of three selected Bacillus cereus sensu lato isolates was investigated in 
three different liquid foods at four different temperatures.  
Twenty Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains isolated from various origins, such as rice, soft ice, 
cooked pasta, vegetable puree, and some commercial products were identified by MALDI-TOF MS 
(Matrix- Assisted Laser Desorption/Ionization Time-of-Flight) and examined for growth ability at 
high holding temperatures mimicking hot-holding scenarios of liquid foods.  
MALDI-TOS MS results showed that 17 out of 20 strains were identified as Bacillus cereus, 
whereas the other three strains (C261, 17544 and 16238) showed varying identification, as B. my-
coides, B. thuringiensis and B. weihenstephanensis. Five strains showed growth in BHI-broth at ≥49 
°C within 24 h, two of those were isolated from rice (T100 and T185) and had max growth temper-
ature of 53 °C. Together with a third isolate, C230 from a RTE food, they were selected for further 
studies. PCR (Polymerase Chain Reaction) confirmed that these isolates potentially were patho-
genic as they contained at least two of the genes coding for enterotoxin production. 
Spores were produced and applied for investigating the growth in BHI-broth, Mornay sauce and as-
paragus soup at 45, 48, 50 and 52 °C within 6 h incubation, mimicking hot-holding of liquid foods, 
e.g. in restaurant or cantina. Samples were taken after heating at 70 °C for 20 min, 3 h after incuba-
tion and 6 h after incubation. All three strains, showed significant (p < 0.05) growth ability in all 
three substrates within 6 h at 45 °C. At 48 °C, two strains (C230, T185) grew in Mornay sauce and 
one (T185) in BHI-broth. At 50 °C, all three strains showed growth potential in Mornay sauce and 
one (T185) in BHI-broth. Finally, at 52 °C very large variations were observed suggesting that 
more replications are needed to obtain reliable results.  
The aim of the study was to investigate growth of Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains in different 
RTE liquid foods within 6 h in order to establish safe hot-holding temperatures. This study showed 
that some Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains can grow at high temperatures of at least 50 °C during 
hot-holding of liquid foods at a constant temperature.

2	    
As the results at 52 °C were too uncertain to make conclusions from, more experiments are needed 
to give a recommendation for safe hot-holding temperatures and times of liquid foods. However, as 
no growth was observed at 54 °C in BHI-broth within 24 h, hot-holding at temperatures above 54 
°C could be considered as safe hot-holding temperature. The performed challenge tests showed, that 
Bacillus cereus sensu lato grew within three-hour incubation, therefore, hot-holding of liquid foods 
should not exceed two-hours.

Preface  
This master thesis has been completed with the Research group for Microbiology and Hygiene at 
Technical University of Denmark in fulfilment of the graduation requirements to obtain a Master of 
Science Degree in Engineering. 
The project has been guided by Senior scientist Tina Beck Hansen from the National Food Institute.   
In connection with the preparation of this project, I would like to thank Senior scientist Tina Beck 
Hansen, for extremely motivating and constructive guidance throughout the project writing process 
and in the laboratory. I would also like to thank Margrethe Carlsen, Resadije Idrizi, and Pia En-
gelsman for their guidance and help in the laboratory.  
The graduation project was prepared by Mehtap Polat Akyol at DTU, autumn 2020. The final pro-
ject was submitted on the 12th. May 2020 in electronic form at DTU Campusnet. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
DTU Lyngby, 12th- May- 2020

7	    
Supervisor 
Tina Beck Hansen, Senior Scientist 
Introduction  
Due to demanding lifestyles, a change in consumer’s eating habits towards easy, ready-to-eat (RTE) 
convenience products is experienced. Consumers are looking for food solutions, that allow them to 
make best use of their free time and spend less time for food planning and cooking their meals. RTE 
foods like prepared vegetables, such as sauces, pasta, rice and soups are an important part of a bal-
anced diet, and today, wide varieties of such products are available on supermarket shelves (1). 
RTE foods goes through various processes, such as washing, trimming, peeling, and slicing, but the 
vegetables are not subjected to heat treatment. The other treatment like ozone treatment or chlorina-
tion do not completely kill the pathogenic bacteria. Therefore, RTE vegetables contamination is a 
major concern for food safety (2). Cooked daily dishes that are kept warm in open cupboards in su-
permarkets or canteens are also very popular, because of their convenience.  
One of the food-safety issues that are associated with RTE foods is growth and/or toxin production 
of Bacillus from the Bacillus cereus group because of inadequate hot holding (3). Temperature con-
trol is a major problem, because of survival of vegetative cells and toxin and possible contamination 
after cooking. Some foods (ex. soups) require to be kept warm at a certain temperature for a long 
time (3-8 hours), but foods kept at high temperatures for a longer time can have significant quality 
losses, such as color, texture, taste and nutritional team. To avoid this, it is necessary to limit the 
time of hot-holding, which maintains the product quality and reducing the risk of foodborne illness 
(4). Improper hot-holding of foods allows the heat resistant endospores of B. cereus that survived 
cooking to germinate, grow, and produce toxin in the product (3). To avoid contamination of RTE 
food with the Bacillus cereus sensu lato group, they must be sterilized at temperatures above 100 ℃ 
to inactivate	 any endospores present. As heating such a high degree will destroy the eating quality 
of many RTE foods, this is not an option for every RTE food.  
Bacteria are suspected to grow faster in liquid than in solid foods (5). Therefore, it may be neces-
sary to have stricter requirements for the hot-holding temperature of liquid RTE foods, such as 
sauces and soups.  
The present study investigated the growth of a selection of strains from the Bacillus cereus sensu 
lato group in liquid substrates that mimicked sauces and soups, in order to establish safe hot-hold-
ing conditions.

8	    
Bacillus spp. 
Bacillus spp. are found ubiquitously and are often defined as a soil bacterium. They are also found 
in spices and in the intestinal flora of different animals. Bacillus spp. are able to form very resistant 
endospores, that can lie in the soil for many years and wait for favorable conditions to growth. The 
pathogenic properties are mainly associated to strains from the B. cereus group and vary between 
different isolates. Due to the fact, that many of isolated from the B. cereus group are harmless, it is 
not a reportable disease (5). Bacillus species can easily be isolated from numerous foods products, 
such as meats, cereal dishes, vegetables, milk products, soups and sauces (1) (6). Due to their re-
sistant endospores they are able to survive different stresses during food production, e.g. heat treat-
ment or drying (1). Bacillus spp. are characterized by the ability to form endospores, which are ex-
tremely acid and salt tolerant. The vegetative form can grow at pH above 4.  
The spores of Bacillus spp. are extremely heat stable and can survive pasteurization temperatures. 
Pasteurization temperatures will only kill the vegetative cells of Bacillus, but their spores will be 
activated and cause germination and growth (7). Due to their activated spores, Bacillus spp. are also 
causative agents in food spoilage, especially in cooked, chilled foods with prolonged shelf- lives.  
For example, activated Bacillus spores can produce an extracellular enzyme, that causes formation 
of a sweet (non-acid) curd, which has a bitter off-flavor on the bottom of the milk containers, which 
lead to the casein to precipitate (7). Hence, in products, such as sauces and other cooked dishes 
cooled slowly down at room temperature, Bacillus can be propagated if the endospore has survived 
the heating process (8) (9). Growth of the Bacillus belonging to the B. cereus group can cause two 
forms of foodborne diseases, the emetic and diarrheal syndrome.  
Besides, its food spoilage and pathogenic properties, Bacillus spp. has also many advantages, as its 
used in many medical, pharmaceutical, agricultural, and industrial processes.  
For example, due to its resistant to heat, chemicals, and radiation Bacillus subtilis is used to validate 
sterilization and fumigation procedures. And certain Bacillus species are important in the natural or 
artificial degradation of waste products, for that reason some Bacillus thuringiensis species are used 
as the active ingredients of insecticides (10).

9	    
Bacillus cereus sensu lato group  
Bacillus cereus is a toxin producing Gram-positive, rod-shaped, spore-forming, facultative anaero-
bic bacterium, which is commonly found in soil and food (11) (5) (12). B. cereus is the most com-
mon group in the genus Bacillus, of the family Bacillaceae (13). The Bacillus cereus group is also 
knowns as B. cereus sensu lato. The B. cereus group consist of eight species, such as B. cereus (B. 
cereus sensu stricto), B. anthracis, B. thuringiensis, B. weihenstephanensis, B. mycoides, B. pseudo-
mycoides, B. cytotoxicus and B. toyonensis (14). 
B. cereus group are distributed into seven different phylogenetic groups (I-VII) (15). They behave 
very differently in their growth potential in relation to pH growth (Table 1), NaCI concentration 
(Table 2), and water activity (aw) (Table 2) (15). This difference between groups may be associated 
with the various toxin producing (16). 
Table 1 shows which phylogenetic group of the Bacillus cereus sensu lato group growing in differ-
ent pH range.

Table 2 illustrates how the different phylogenetic groups belonging to Bacillus cereus sensu lato 
group behave in different NaCI concentration and water activity.  
B. weihenstephanensis, B. mycoides, and B. pseudomycoides have not been the cause of food poi-
soning, and their toxic potential is still uncertain (14). B. cytotoxicus is a new species, which is dis-
covered in 2013. This species contains cytK-1 gene and can growth up to 50 - 53 ℃. B. toyonensis 
is validated in 2014 and it is isolated from soil in Japan. B. toyonensis is used as Toyocerin ® pow-
der in animal feed (14). B. anthracis belonging to group III and is known as human pathogen, that 
cause anthrax (15), which is acute fatal disease in animals and in humans (14). Spore and crystal 
toxin preparations from B. thuringiensis are used as a commercial insecticide pathogen and is pre-
sent in various food (18).  
The phylogenetic group II, III, IV, V, and VII are associated with outbreaks of foodborne illnesses. 
Groups II, V and VI are cold-tolerant, whereas groups III, IV and VII are more thermotolerant (16).  
Table 3 shows which phylogenetic group the Bacillus cereus sensu lato group belongs to, as well as 
their growth characteristics.

12	    
Spore sporulation and germination  
Bacillus species consist of two different states, such as vegetative cell and the endospore. B. cereus 
life cycle consist of three different stages, such as sporulation, germination and vegetative growth 
(19). B. cereus spores can survive in its dormant spore state in many years. The endospore produc-
tion is a very important survival characteristic of B. cereus, which is an inactive form of the bacte-
rium. The spore formation is triggered by a lack of nutrients (20). The first step of spore formation 
process (Figure 1) is an unequal cell division of the vegetative cell. This forms the smaller part and 
the larger mother cell part. Then, the forespore is encapsulated in the mother cell (19). 
During sporulation, morphological and biological changes occur. The spore is covered into two lay-
ers, which are the spore cortex and the spore coat. These layers are separated by an outer membrane 
(Figure 1). The spore coat has a multilayered structure that encases the spore and is composed of 
many cross-linked polypeptide layers. The spore coat provides spores with resistance against exoge-
nous lytic enzymes, organic solvents, and oxidative chemicals. It acts primarily as a permeability 
barrier. The cortex contains peptidoglycan and is important for the protection of spore core dehy-
dration. The core contains the cytoplasmatic proteins, ribosomes and DNA.  
It contains a water percentage of 30 – 50 %, which is lower than the 70 – 88 % in the vegetative 
cell. The spore accumulates around 10 % of dry weight of Pyridine- 2, 6- dicarboxylic acid (dipico-
linic acid or DPA) in the core. DPA is related to the core dehydration and plays a role in heat re-
sistance and UV resistance. The spore also has huge number of divalent cations (calcium).  
 
Figure	  1:	  Life	  cycle	  of	  Bacillus	  cereus	  and	  a	  close-­‐up	  of	  its	  endospore.	  Modified	  from	  (21)	  and	  	  (22)

for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
13	    
The vegetative cell can be transformed from the dormant spore, by three different steps, which con-
sist of activation, germination and outgrowth (21). Activation of spores can take place by using ap-
propriate conditions of heat, pH or chemical exposure. The first stage occurs when the nutrients 
such as amino acids sugars, and purine nucleosides are registered by the germinations receptors 
(GRs) that are located in the inner membrane of the spore (Figure 1). As the spore begins to germi-
nate, H+, K+, Na+ and Ca+2, are released which raises the pH of the spore from 6.5 to 7.7. Thereafter, 
lytic enzymes are activated and the protective spore peptidoglycan cortex is degraded. The second 
stage occurs when the DPA (pyridine 2, 6-dicarboxylsyre) is degraded and released followed by re-
hydration of the spore core. During the rehydration, biochemical processes become activated. The 
third stage occurs at the onset of the incipient vegetative cell after spore coat hydrolysis (19) (23). 
The most used method is a sub lethal heat shock, for example, heating spores for 10 min at 80 °C. If 
the spores are activated before exposure to nutrients that cause germination, it will activate more 
rapidly and completely (24). Then, germination of spores can take place when dormancy is broken. 
Thereafter, vegetative growth occurs by binary symmetric fission cell growth, when nutrients are 
available (21) (25).

14	    
Toxins  
B. cereus is recognized as a causative agent of gastrointestinal diseases connected to food-poisoning 
caused by the enterotoxins and emetic toxins. The emetic toxins cause nausea and vomiting (26) 
and the enterotoxins cause diarrhea. In addition to the food poisoning, B. cereus has also been im-
plicated in infections of the eye, respiratory tract, and in wounds, as well as systematic infections 
(12) (27). According to the European Food Safety Authority (EFSA, 2005), the required concentra-
tion of B. cereus to cause disease is 105 CFU/g, but 103 and 104 CFU/g were also recorded as a food 
poisoning outbreak (28).  
 
Enterotoxin  
 
B. cereus sensu lato produce different proteins known as enterotoxins (29). The diarrheal poisoning 
is caused by heat and acid-labile enterotoxins (18), which is caused by B. cereus spores, that germi-
nate in the contaminated food cause enterotoxins produced during vegetative growth in the small 
intestine (27). Then few hours after ingestion of food the toxin cause watery diarrhea with ab-
dominal cramps, and rectal spasms (30), appearing after an incubation time within 8 - 16 h, that 
takes about 12-24 h (31). The late onset of symptoms is caused by the transit time of the pathogen 
through the stomach and the small intestine. The other reason for the late onset is caused by the 
time required for the growth of B. cereus cells in the intestine. The enterotoxins can be inactivated 
by heating at 56 ℃ for 5 min. They are also inactivated in the stomach because of the low pH (30). 
Foods related with the diarrheal- type usually contain high numbers of B. cereus between 105-108 g 
food (32). The enterotoxins are unstable at low pH and are degraded by digestive enzymes. Pre-
formed enterotoxins in food will be degraded during passage through the stomach, and cannot cause 
illness after ingestion (33).  
The diarrheal food poisoning is associated with two protein complexes, including, hemolysin BL 
(Hbl) and nonhaemolytic enterotoxin (Nhe) and two single proteins cytotoxin CytK (32) and entero-
toxin entFM (34). There are two types of CytK gene, which is renamed to CytK-1, and the second 
form, is CytK2. CytK2 have lower toxicity than CytK-1 (14). The genes responsible for Hbl consist 
of four different genes, such as hblA, hblB, hblC and hblD (35). The Nhe (nonhaemolytic) toxin 
composed of three encoded genes, such as nheA, nheB and nheC (36). Hbl consist of three compo-
nents, these are B, L1 and L2. NheA is similar to L2, NheB is similar to L1 and NheC is similar to a 
binding protein B (32) (35). 
==PAGE:16==
DTU	  National	  Food	  Institute	  Research	  Group	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  s093510	  Mehtap	  Polat	  Akyol	  	  
for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
15	    
The diarrheal food poisoning is caused by the production of enterotoxin complexes Hbl and Nhe, 
which are produced by B. cereus, B. thuringiensis and B. mycoides. B. anthracis produces the gene 
Nhe (37). As mentioned before (section The Bacillus cereus group) Cry proteins are used as insecti-
cide, because of the receptors in the insect gut. These receptors are not present in the mammalian 
gut and therefore are not dangerous for the humans. However, Cry proteins, produced by the bacte-
ria B. thuringiensis strains are able to produce other toxins, which can affect humans, that have the 
same diarrhea –causing enterotoxins as produced by B. cereus (38). 
 
Emetic toxin  
 
The emetic toxins consist of CES and CER genes (39). CES gene stands for cereulides, which are 
cyclic dodecadepsipeptide toxins (40). Cereulides have a ring structure of three repeats of four 
amino acids: [D-O-Leu-D-Ala-L-O-Val-L-Val]3 (Figure 2). The emetic toxin, CES gene is produced 
by B. cereus, B. anthracis and B. thuriengiensis (41). 
 
 
The emetic-type outbreak is characterized by the toxin cereulide, which is pre-formed in the food 
(42). Fried, cooked rice and pasta, that has been left at room temperature for a long time and then 
eaten, is usually associated with the emetic-type outbreak (43) (44). Cereulide is also found in food 
of animal origin like meat, eggs and dairy products (30). Foods associated with B. cereus emetic in-
toxication contain at least 105 CFU/g (45).  
The emetic toxins are a heat- and acid-stable (27). They are resistant to autoclaving and heat treat-
ment at 121 ℃ (90 min) and extremely stable at pH between 2-11 (30) (24). The emetic toxin is 
produced in high amounts at temperatures between 20- 25 ℃ (30).

16	    
The disease caused by emetic toxins is characterized by nausea, vomiting, and abdominal cramps, 
but it can also cause diarrhea, due to Bacillus also producing enterotoxins (30). Compared to the di-
arrheal symptoms of Bacillus species, the emetic toxin has a shorter onset of symptoms, which oc-
curs after 30 min to 6 h after consumption of the food (40).

17	    
Bacillus cereus sensu lato outbreak  
B. cereus is classified as a hazard group two microorganism of The European Commission, because 
of its ability to cause infections in humans (European Commission Council Directive 93/88/EEC 
1993) (30). The comparison of B. cereus outbreaks in different countries is impossible, since their 
method of tracking spores is different. For example, Bacillus outbreaks in Japan, North America 
and Europe range from 1 to 47 % and from 0,7 to 33 % of cases (29). Outbreaks of illness caused 
by Bacillus cereus in Denmark from 2016 was 2 % of the reported foodborne illness (46). The num-
ber of outbreaks increased to 6,3 % in 2017 (47). 
There are two types of outbreaks, which consist of the diarrheal-type outbreak and the emetic-type 
outbreak. Neither the emetic nor the diarrheal type of outbreak is life threatening. The pathogenic 
properties of B. cereus vary between different isolates. It is only a few B. cereus isolates that lead to 
serious illness and many of them are quite harmless (48). Table 4 shows B. cereus outbreaks that 
have been in EU between 2007 and 2012.

18	    
Bacillus cereus sensu lato contamination in foods   
B. cereus sensu lato are ubiquitous worldwide distributed foodborne pathogen (52), which are pre-
sent in numerous foods, such as eggs, RTE foods, spices meat products, rice, dairy products, and so 
on, and therefore it is impossible to find a food that are free of these microorganisms (53). Contami-
nation of foods with Bacillus cereus sensu lato is generally low. Due to the heat resistance of Bacil-
lus cereus sensu lato spores they can survive various treatment during food production, such as dry-
ing and heat treatment. According to the study of Rosenquist et al. (2005) (54) around 48 of 901 
samples of RTE foods at the Danish market had B. cereus bacteria between 103 - 104 CFU/g, and 
0.5 % of the samples which was determined in starchy, cooked products and in fresh cucumbers and 
tomatoes had counts above 104 CFU/g-1 (54). In another study, the production of an emetic toxin 
(cereulide) was investigated in various food sources and they detected low levels of emetic toxin in 
13 of 14 food samples such as milk, egg, meat, vinegar, mayonnaise, noodle, rice, sushi, mashed 
potato, and spaghetti with a concentration rate between 0,01 to 1,28 µg/g. They have shown growth 
at 20, 30 and 35 °C with an incubation time of 24 h (55).  
Bacillus cereus sensu lato in hot-hold foods  
Food poisoning/illnesses is caused by eating contaminated foods with bacteria. Bacillus is a major 
problem due to its resistant endospores. Bacillus spores may be present on various raw and cooked 
foods. Due to the ability of the spores survive at high cooking temperatures. Cooked foods need to 
be served hot or cooled quickly to prevent the bacteria from growing. A slow cooling after heat 
treatment can allow the growth of bacteria causing illnesses (56). 
Heat-treated food is in the danger zone if food is stored within a temperature range 5-65 ° C, it can 
cause possibility to microorganism being multiply in the food (57). In order to avoid microbial 
growth in food, that can cause spoilage or food poisoning, the food have to be fully heat treated to a 
temperature of at least 75 ºC. This rule also applies to restaurants or institutional kitchens, that need 
to reheat the refrigerated and prepared food (58). If the food is served hot in a restaurant or in a can-
teen, the food must have a constant hot-holding temperature of at least 65 ºC without limitation time 
(58). Companies that prepare the food and store it for later, the food must be cooled down quickly 
to avoid growth of bacteria. It is recommended by the Food authority in Denmark (Fødevarestyrel-
sen 2015), that the cooling of heat treated food should decrease from 65 to 10 ° C within a maxi-
mum of three hours (59). Bacillus cereus can be present in food in low amount without posing any 
health risk (60).

19	    
According to the study in 1997, the Veterinary and Food Directorate has conducted in collaboration 
with Denmark’s 32 food control units the occurrence of Bacillus in hot-hold food generally was 
low. The samples were taken from supermarkets, slaughterhouses, restaurants, and retail corpora-
tion. A total of 1307 samples of hot-cooked dishes were taken and only 10 of them were positive, 
with an average concentration of 1500 CFU/g (min. 100 – max. 1100 CFU/g) (61).

20	    
Materials and methods  
The aim of the study was to investigate the growth ability of Bacillus cereus sensu lato in hot-hold 
liquid RTE foods, such as sauces and soups. This was achieved by first selecting three different 
strains from a pool of 20 presumptive Bacillus cereus, for application in challenge tests. MALDI-
TOF MS (Matrix- Assisted Laser Desorption/Ionization Time-of-Flight) methods were used to 
identify, which of the 20 strains belonged to the different species of the Bacillus cereus sensu lato 
group. Then, PCR (Polymerase Chain Reaction) method were used to test, which toxin genes the 
various Bacillus strains contained. Growth on agar plates and in BHI-broth were determined for a 
temperature range from 5 to 55 °C. Three of the strains able to grow in the high temperature range 
were selected and spores were produced for the challenge tests. Hereafter, growth was investigated 
in pretests in BHI-broth. In the main tests, growth was investigated in a commercial soup (aspara-
gus soup) and a commercial sauce (Mornay sauce). Finally, data analyses were performed. Before 
starting the laboratory work, risk assessment was performed, which can be seen in Appendix 1.  
Overview of the study can be seen in Figure 3.

22	    
Selection of strains for challenge test  
 
A total of twenty B. cereus sensu lato strains isolated from different RTE foods, vegetables, and 
some commercial insecticide were used (from DTU Food strain collection), in order to determine 
suitable strains, that can grow at high temperatures as the purpose was to examine hot-holding of 
RTE foods within six hours to investigate the B. cereus growth. Source for the different strains can 
be seen in Table 14. 
Due to the difficulty to distinguish the B. cereus colonies on BA and TSA plates, as other bacteria 
also grew sometimes, MALDI-TOF MS was used to give more clear results. The conventional 
method PCR, was used to test the different toxin genes belonging to the B. cereus sensu lato group.  
The necessary strains from the different Bacillus cereus groups were selected by examine growth 
on BA-plates and in BHI-broth at different temperatures (5-55 °C). Strains (17544, 11129, T101, 
T185 and T100) that grew between 49 °C and 55 °C required an additional treatment to be sure of 
growth of B. cereus in the BHI-broth. It was done by measuring optical density (OD) for the se-
lected samples after incubation overnight in water bath. OD- measurements can be seen in Table 16. 
Since the temperature of the incubator may vary by opening the incubator door or by other faults, it 
was safer to investigate the growth temperature using water bath. By the performed experiments, an 
assumption was made of which strains belong to the different phylogenetic groups of B. cereus (Ta-
ble 3). 
Finally, the necessary strains which grew to 53 °C were selected, which were T100, and T185. In 
order to use it for the hot-holding pre-test experiment, spores were prepared for the selected strains. 
Afterwards, the number of spores in the selected strains were decided after heat-treatment at 80 °C 
in 10 min., which is explained more detailed in section, Spore production. 
The pre-test hot-holding experiment in BHI-broth have been done by using the selected spores 
T100 and T185. Another strain like C230 was also included, because it grew to 49 °C in BHI broth 
and in cooked pasta at 47 °C (performed by another student) (62). To perform this pre-test four dif-
ferent temperatures were chosen, such as 45, 48, 50 and 52 °C, which were within the Food Author-
ity’s requirement to hot-holding the food temperature is between 10 - 65 °C (63). The incubation 
time for the samples were performed at T0 (without heat treatment), T20,min (at 70 °C) T3 (3h) and T6 
(6h). Six hours was included because after three hours of incubation in water bath, it was impossi-
ble to see some changes with the spores growth.

23	    
MALDI-­‐TOF	  MS	  Identification	  	  
MALDI-TOF MS is an abbreviation of Matrix-Assisted Laser Desorption / Ionization Time-of-
Flight Mass Spectrometry, which is used to identify different bacterial species (64).  
To identify the strains, each strain was first streaked on sheep blood agar, BA- plates (BA, Oxoid, 
England) and practical identification by MALDI-TOF MS of the samples were performed by a la-
boratory technician. 
MALDI-TOF MS gives three scores (Table 5) for identification as an expression of comparison to 
the database. The higher the score value the higher the similarity to the database. A score value of ≥ 
2.00 is considered as a high probability of identification. 
Table	  5:	  Score	  values	  for	  MALDI-­‐TOF	  MS	  identification	  (65)	  
 
 
 
 
 
All the confirmed Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains were tested for possible pathogenic potential by 
detection of toxin genes using PCR.

Interpretation

24	    
Conventional	  PCR	  (Polymerase	  Chain	  Reaction)	  	  
 
Conventional PCR, using six toxin genes, cytK, nheA, CER, hblC, entFM and CES (38), was ap-
plied for characterization of the pathogenic potential of the isolates.  
 
Pre-­‐test	  for	  the	  CES	  gene	  	  
First, the CES gene primers was used to verify that the PCR worked. The CES primer, was designed 
based on multifunctional proteins NRPS (nonribosomal peptide synthetase) (66) (67), which is re-
sponsible for emetic toxin synthesis (68). This has been done by comparing the CER gene’s results 
from a former student’s results. In order to compare the CER gene with the CES gene, three positive 
control strains were used, which were 17533 (contains both the CES and CER genes), 16238 and 
17544. The positive control strains were selected from another study (69) as it has four toxin genes, 
such as, nheA, hblC, CER and cry3 genes. The data obtained from this experiment is shown in Ta-
ble 12: Testing of the CES gene.  
 
Cell	  lysis	  	  
Cell lysis have to be performed to determine the purity of the DNA, before using PCR. 
Procedure for lysing the cells in the strains: 
-  
In total 20 strains were grown on tryptic soya agar (TSA) plates at 30 ℃ for 24 h. 
-  
One bacteria colony was transferred into 10 ml BHI media in a centrifuge tubes, and after-
wards incubated at 30 ℃ for 24 h. 
-  
1 ml was transferred into 1,5 ml Eppendorf tube 
-  
Then, it was centrifuged at 4000 x for 5 min 
-  
Afterwards, the supernatant was removed and added 200 µl 10X TE buffer into the Eppen-
dorf tube and dissolved the pellet 
-  
In order to break the cells, the mixture was heated at 98 ℃ in heat block 
-  
Afterwards, the mixture was centrifuged at 10.000 X g for 5 min 
-  
The supernatant was transferred into a new Eppendorf tube 
After cell lysis, the strains DNA concentration were measured on a NanoDropTM spectrophotome-
ter. The DNA concentrations are presented in Table 11.

25	    
Preparation	  for	  Conventional	  PCR	  	  
 
The developed conventional PCR was used for detection of toxin genes in 20 different Bacillus ce-
reus sensu lato strains. Calculations of the amounts of components for the master mix were based 
on the product information from Thermo Scientific (70). Primers for six toxin genes (cytK, nheA, 
CER, hblC, entFM and CES) were used (Table 6). The primer sets used in this study were obtained 
from the Institute of Food Technologists (66).

A volume of 25 µL (Table 6) mastermix was transferred in PCR-strips. Setup of samples in PCR-
strips can be seen in Appendix 6b. The PCR cycling condition for the detection of emetic- toxin and 
diarrheal toxin producing Bacillus cereus were obtained from this study Kim et al. (2010) (68). 
There were two different PCR cycles, one for the CES gene, and the other for the genes that are 
cytK, nheA, CER, hblC, and entFM. The PCR conditions for the CES gene was as follows:  a single 
denaturation step of 15 min at 95 ℃, a step cycle program set for 5 cycle (with a cycle consisting of 
denaturation at 95 ℃, for 1 min, annealing at 53 ℃, for 1 min 15 sec and extension at 72 ℃ for 50 
sec). The second step cycle program set for 25 cycle (with a cycle consisting of denaturation at 95 
℃ for 1 min, annealing at 58 ℃ for 1 min and 15 sec and extension at 72 ℃ for 50 sec) and a final 
extension step for 5 min at 72 ℃ (Appendix 7a: The PCR program for the CES gene). 
The PCR conditions for the other genes cytK, nheA, CER, hblC, and entFM were as follows: a sin-
gle denaturation step of 10 min at 95 ℃, a step cycle program set for 35 cycle (with a cycle consist-
ing of denaturation at 94 ℃ for 1 min, annealing at 54 ℃ for 1 min and extension at 72 ℃ for 1 
min) and a final extension step for 5 min at 72 ℃ (Appendix 7b: The PCR program for the cytK, 
nheA, CER, hblC, and entFM genes).

26	    
The	  preparation	  of	  the	  gel	  electrophoresis	  	  
 
The agarose gel was prepared mixing 1.5 g Agarose (SeaKem® LE Agarose, LOT no: 
0000367828) with 100 mL of Tris-acetate (TAE) buffer. Thereafter, the mixture was heated in a mi-
crowave oven until the agarose was dissolved. Finally, 10 µL of DNA gel stain (SYBR® Safe, LOT 
no: 489230) was added into the mixture. Afterwards, the gel was poured into sled (comb inserted 
into mold to make wells). The gel was cooled at room temperature to solidify. Thereafter, the gel 
was placed in and electrophoresis chamber and added prepared 1.5 % TAE buffer to cover the gel. 
Then, the comb was removed and 6 µL of the 100 bp DNA Ladder (LOT no: 10051051) was added 
into the first well of the gel, and then 10 µL of each sample was added into the respective. The gel 
was run at 100 volts for approximately 1 hour, and took a picture was taken using Gel Doc 2000 
(BIO-RAD) with the Quantity One program. 
Once the DNA fragments have been separated, the gel was analyzed and interpreted depending on 
the sizes of bands are found on it. Table 7 is used to interpret the gel electrophoresis.  
Setup of each samples in gel-electrophoresis are listed in Appendix 6c1: First setup of samples in 
gel-electrophoresis, and in Appendix 6c2: Second setup of samples in gel-electrophoresis

different	  article	  as	  following	  (66)	  (68).

27	    
Growth	  on	  BA-­‐plates	  	  
In order to predict the affiliation to specific phylogenetic groups and to select the suitable strains for 
the challenge test, all 20 Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains with a pathogenic potential were streak-
plated onto the BA, and then incubated overnight, or for up to three days, at different temperatures 
between 30-45 °C. Afterwards, higher temperatures up to 49 °C and lower temperatures down to 5 
°C were tested depending on prior growth results for the individual strain. Appendix 4: Growth ex-
periment in BHI-broth and on BA, indicates, which strains were incubated at which temperatures.  
All Bacillus cereus group strains were used in the next experiment analyzing the growth in a liquid 
substrate, BHI.  
 
Growth	  in	  BHI	  broth	  	  	  
Due to the purpose of the study to investigate the Bacillus cereus sensu lato group growth in liquid 
hot holding RTE foods, all strain’s growth conditions were tested again in BHI-broth as it repre-
sents a liquid food (ex. Sauces), due to its good nutrition content.  
To select the reliable strains for the challenge test, strains were seeded in a 15 mL glass tube, which 
contained 10 mL of Brain Heart Infusion broth (BHI, Oxoid, England) and incubated overnight at 
different temperature between 30-55 °C, which is demonstrated in Appendix 4: Growth experiment 
in BHI-broth and on BA.  
The strains that grew to 55 °C in BHI-broth were again incubated at 55 °C by using a different 
method, in order to be sure, the growth was not due to a contamination in BHI-broth, but B. cereus 
growth. The BHI-broth was first heated in the incubator for 20 min and then seeded immediately (to 
avoid cooling of the BHI-broth) to the strains and incubated at 55 °C overnight. 
In the next experiment, the strains, that grow at high temperatures were investigated as the aim of 
the project is about examining Bacillus cereus sensu lato group growth in hot-holding RTE liquid 
food, that is hot-hold at temperatures between 45, 48, 50 and 52 °C.  
Five different strains were selected to measure OD with a spectrophotometer (ThermoSpectronic) at 
600 nm to detect possible growth of Bacillus cereus sensu lato, as they showed growth in BHI-
broth. To be sure, that the growth was not due to contamination of BHI-broth, the water bath exper-
iment was performed until there was no growth.

28	    
The selected strains (17544, 11129, T101, T185 and T100) have been incubated overnight at differ-
ent temperature, at 49 °C, 50 °C, 51 °C, 52 °C, 53 °C, and 54 °C in BHI broth on a shaking water 
bath to achieve bacteria growth. Afterwards OD600 (before/after) was measured to see the difference 
in growth of the different strains.  
Differences of OD600 measuring (Appendix 5: OD- measurement) was considered as growth in 
BHI. Then, strains that showed growth were streaked onto BA and incubated at 30 °C overnight, to 
confirm the growth in BHI. 
 
Overview of the experiment can be seen in Figure 4.

29	    
Challenge tests  
In this section the method of the challenge tests will be presented. First the method of spore produc-
tion of the selected strains will be presented, and then, the pre-tests in BHI-broth performed at dif-
ferent temperatures with the selected strains will be explained. Finally, the challenge tests in foods 
will be described with subsequent data processing.  
 
Spore	  production	  	  
Based on results from the OD experiment, two strains (T100 and T185) were selected for use in the 
challenge tests. Spores of these strains were produced as illustrated in Figure 5. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Streak-plate the selected bacteria cultures be-
longing to T100 and T185 on TSA-plates and 
incubate overnight at 30 °C  
Propagate the cultures twice in 10 mL BHI-
broth and incubate overnight at 30 °C 
Distribute 1 mL of the propagated cultures 
with a drigalski spatula on TSA-plate and in-
cubate in 5 days at 30 °C 
Harvest the spores by adding 1 mL milli-Q-
water to the TSA-plate and transfer the col-
ony suspension to a 15 mL centrifuge tube, 
then fill the tube up to 10 mL  
Centrifuge the tubes for 15 
min at 20 °C with 3800 rpm  
Wash the pellet 
(spore) twice with 
milli-Q-water  
Mix pellet with 0.9 % NaCl 
(10 mL) and store in the re-
frigerator (8 °C)

30	    
 
In order to determine the concentration (CFU/ml) of spores, 1 mL of spore solution was transferred 
into 15 mL centrifuge tubes and mixed with 9 mL 0.9 % NaCI. The tube was immersed at 80 ℃ in 
10 min in order to inactivate any vegetative cells in the solution and activate the spores and then 
cooled. A 10-fold dilution series up to 10-7 of each strain (T100 and T185) was prepared, by trans-
ferring a volume of 0.1 ml of the spore in to the 1.5 ml Eppendorf tubes containing 0.9 % NaCI.  
All dilutions were plated on TSA plates by spot test (3x10 µL). Afterwards, plates were incubated 
at room temperature (22-23 °C) overnight.  
The selected strains were used to investigate growth in BHI-broth in the next experiment, as is rep-
resents liquid food.  
Pre-­‐tests	  in	  BHI-­‐broth	  	  
The aim of pre-tests experiment in BHI-broth was to investigate whether Bacillus cereus sensu lato. 
strains grow in a time interval between 0 to 6 hours (20 min, 3 h, and 6 h), to investigate how long it 
takes for Bacillus cereus sensu lato group strains to grow in foods. Sampling takes place after 3 h 
heat treatment first, since food that is hot hold more than three hours below 65 °C is discarded (57), 
due to growth of bacteria. Sampling after 6-hour heat treatment was included in this study, as three 
hours was not enough to assess the growth and germination of cells.  
Two strains (T100 and T185) were selected for the challenge tests as they showed growth at high 
temperature up to 53 °C both in BHI-broth (in water bath/in incubator, Table 15) and on BA plate 
(Table 14). The strain C230 was also included in this experiment, as it had shown growth in a food, 
cooked pasta (62).  
The pre-tests were performed in duplicate at four different temperatures, which were 45, 48, 50 and 
52 ºC. This experiment was performed with two water baths, one was set to 70 ºC and another set to 
the desired hot-holding temperature. The spore solutions were diluted in 108 to a concentration of 
107 and 40 µL was inoculated into 40 mL BHI-broth in 100-mL blue-cap bottles (Figure 6), which 
was heated at 70 in 20 min to eliminate any vegetative cells in the solution. The samples were trans-
ferred directly into the other water bath, simulating a food being placed for hot- holding immedi-
ately after heat treatment and incubated for up to 6 h. Before heat treatment, after heat treatment and 
after 3 and 6 h incubation, a dilution row of 100, 10-1, 10-2 and 10-3 was prepared and made spot 
tested (3x10 µL) on TSA-plates to enumerate. A control was prepared consisting of 40 mL BHI-
broth, which was incubated with the samples, to check possible contamination of BHI-broth.

32	    
 
Main	  tests	  in	  foods	  	  
 
Challenge tests were conducted to investigate how the selected strains (T100, T185 and C230) be-
haved in RTE foods within six hours by hot-holding at the same temperatures as used in the pre-
tests. For that reason, two RTE foods were purchased from Netto and Føtex the day before the ex-
periment was performed (Table 8). One of the samples was asparagus soup and the other one was 
Mornay sauce. Both of the samples containing only stabilizer and emulsifier as additives. It was im-
portant to purchase samples without preservatives as they could inhibit Bacillus cereus sensu lato 
growth. Both samples were liquid as the purpose of the study was to examine Bacillus growth in 
liquid RTE foods.

Before starting the main tests, pH was measured for the two foods using pH indicator strips (Merck, 
Neutralit®).  
Salt content for the two samples was calculated based on the declared nutritional content (Table 8). 
The salt concentration was calculated by applying equation below: 
 
Salt- in-water (%) = 100 x ((salt pr. 100 g) / (water pr. 100 g + salt pr. 100 g))

33	    
 
Duplicates samples of 40 g of each food were weighed in separate 100-mL blue cap bottles. A vol-
ume of 40 µL of the three selected spore solutions were first heated separately at 70 ºC in 20 min in 
water bath, while the food sample was in the other water bath, which was set to the desired hot-
holding temperatures (either 45, 48, 50 or 52 ºC). After heat treatment of the strains, they were 
added into the samples, to obtain a start concentration of approximately 4 log (CFU/m). Before the 
samples were put in to the water bath, 10-fold dilution rows were made one by one (to avoid cool-
ing of the samples, that took a maximum of one minute), by transferring a volume of 100 µL of the 
sample in to 1.5 mL Eppendorf tubes containing 0.9 mL of 0.9 % NaCl. Dilution rows were made 
up to 10-3 of each sample containing different spores and the sample. Then, the samples were put 
back in to the water bath with the pre-set temperature. The same process was repeated again after 3 
h and 6 h with a dilution row up to 10-3. After each sampling (0 h, 3 h and 6 h) each dilution of sam-
ples was plated on TSA agar plate by spot test (3 x 10 µL) and incubated at room temperature (22-
23 ºC) overnight. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Spores: C230, T100 and T185 at 70 ºC in 
20 min in the one water bath  
Samples asparagus soup or mornay sauce in 
the other water bath (45, 48, 50, 52 ºC)  
Transferred 40 µl of spores into the sam-
ples and diluted samples up to 10-3 by 
transferring 100 µl of the sample into the 
Eppendorf tube containing 0.9 % NaCI 
Sampling process repeated after 3h and 
6h with the same dilution row 10-1, 10-2, 
and 10-3 and samples plated on TSA plate 
by spot testing (3x10 µL) and then incu-
bated at room temperature overnight

34	    
Results and Discussion  
In this section the results of the experiments will be presented and discussed. In order to select relia-
ble strains for the challenge test, all 20 Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains identified by using 
MALDI-TOF MS is presented in Table 10.   
In order to see, how the Bacillus cereus sensu lato behaves in solid and liquid media, it was neces-
sary to streak-plate given strains on BA-plates and seed the strains in BHI-broth.  
Results for the growth experiments on BA-plates and in BHI-broth are illustrated in Appendix 4: 
Growth experiment in BHI-broth and on BA-plates. The OD measurements for the selected strains 
(T185, T100, 17544, T101 and 11129) will be introduced, which is shown in Table 16.  
The assumed phylogenetic groups (Table 17) of B. cereus were completed after growth experiments 
(Appendix 4: Growth experiment in BHI-broth and on BA-plates) and OD-measurements. Spores of 
the selected spores (C230, T100 and T185) have been produced to apply it in the pre-test experi-
ment in BHI-broth and afterwards in the main-test in foods. Concentrations of spores for the se-
lected strains have been calculated after making spot-test on TSA-plates. The determined concentra-
tion was approx. 8 log (CFU/ml) (Appendix: 2). Results for the pre-test is shown in section, pre-test 
in BHI.  
Results for the main tests in foods is also presented, which consist of pH-measurement for the pur-
chased product, as asparagus soup and Mornay sauce, and subsequently salt content calculations for 
these products.  
Results for the experiment conducted with the selected spores and the purchased foods were sum-
marized in Table 21.

36	    
MALDI-­‐TOF	  MS	  Identification	  	  
The MALDI-TOF MS identification method accomplished in identifying 20 Bacillus cereus sensu 
lato strains.  
The dominant bacterium was Bacillus cereus, which was identified in 17 out of 20 strains, that had 
a score in between 1.82-2.49, which is considered as a high probability of identification at species 
level. The three remaining isolates detected content of three different Bacillus spp.  
Table 10 shows identification results completed by the MALDI-TOF MS, that presents four species 
of Bacillus.  
 
The strain 16238 (Commercial product, Dipel), showed presence of three different Bacillus spp., 
such as B. cereus, B. mycoides and B. thuringiensis. The other three different bacteria species, con-
sisted of B. weihenstephanensis and B. mycoides belongin to the strain 17544 (Commercial product, 
Foray). Another strain, containing three different Bacillus spp. was C261 (isolated from classic la-
sagna), which consisted of B. weihenstephanensis, B. cereus, and B. mycoides.

plated	  on	  TSA-­‐plates	  and	  incubated	  overnight	  at	  30 ℃. Identification on MALDI-­‐TOF	  MS	  was	  done	  by	  a	  laboratory.

for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
37	    
According to the Thema report from DMU (Danmarks Miljøundersøgelser 2000) (71) B. weihen-
stephanensis grows at 5 and 7 °C, but in the present study no growth was achieved below 10 °C for 
the strains 17544 and C261. Although, the MALDI-TOF MS scores for this identification were 
more than 2, which is considered as high identification, it may be that the isolate does not contain 
the bacterium.  
The other strain 16238, contained B. thuringiensis, that have a growth temperature between 8-42 °C 
in this study, but in another study it showed, B. thuringiensis belongs to five phylogenetic groups of 
Bacillus cereus sensu lato, that are, II, III, IV, V and VI, which means that the bacterium belongs to 
both the mesophilic group and the psychrotrophic group, that can grow between 5-45 °C (15). This 
temperature disagreement between studies may explain, that it can be an incorrect identification, 
due to the absence of some species in the MALDI-TOF MS data library (72) .  
Various studies have been performed on MALDI-TOF MS identification of Bacillus cereus sensu 
lato group, which have shown different results. According to the study performed in 2013 (Fernan-
dez-No et al., 2012), the identification of Bacillus spp. within the Bacillus cereus sensu lato group 
was not possible by using commercial databases by MALDI-TOF MS (73). Whereas in another 
study, the MALDI-TOF MS method worked very well, as it was possible to identify 120 out of 121 
tested strains, were correctly (74).  
The reason for the varying identification by MALDI-TOF MS was demonstrated in a previously 
study (75), as the spores, which are mixed with the vegetative cells, identification of Bacillus cereus 
sensu lato strains was particularly difficult, which may be the same reason in the present study.  
Identification with MALDI-TOF MS of strains, such as 16238, 17544 and C261 were very uncer-
tain, as results by MALDI-TOF MS, showed three different suggestions, like Bacillus weihensteph-
anensis, B. thuringiensis and Bacillus mycoides (Table 10). In a previous study, it was determined 
that, some strains of Bacillus mycoides were difficult to distinguish from B. cereus and other strains 
of Bacillus mycoides were confused with Bacillus weihenstephanensis, which made it hard to dis-
tinguish between B. cereus sensu lato strains (76).  
Another study confirmed the close relatedness, because of the high genetic similarity among the 
type strains of B. thuringiensis, Bacillus weihenstephanensis, Bacillus cereus and Bacillus my-
coides, which could also be the reason of the varying identification of the strains (77).

38	    
 
Conventional	  PCR	  (Polymerase	  Chain	  Reaction)	  	  
 
In this section, results are presented for identification of the various toxin genes (cytK, nheA, CER, 
hblC, entFM and CES), which are present in Bacillus cereus sensu lato group. All 20 strains iso-
lated from different sources were included in the PCR as they may contain the genes.  
Table 11 shows the concentration and purity of the strains 21792, 11129, T100 and C230 measured 
on a NanoDropTM spectrophotometer. The ratio of absorbance at A260/280 to assess the purity must 
be higher than 1.8 to perform PCR (78) (79). It is important to know the DNA concentration for in-
terpreting the PCR gel whether there is a band or not, and most important for the PCR, because if 
there is no DNA, then it does not make sense to run PCR.  
Due to the time limitations and the NanoDropTM spectrophotometer did not work, it was impossible 
to measure the concentration of DNA for all strains. The measured strains can be seen in Table 11.

and compare the PCR results with another student’s result. As seen in the picture of agarose gel 
electrophoresis (Figure 8), similar results were obtained. The emetic toxin gene in the control strain 
17533 was verified with the two different primer set- CES and the CER gene.

41	    
PCR	  detection	  for	  the	  six	  different	  toxin	  genes	  (CES,	  CER,	  hblC,	  nheA,	  hblC	  and	  cytK)	  
 
The presence of six toxin genes (CES, CER, hblC, nheA, hblC and cytK) was detected by the con-
ventional PCR in all 20 different Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains isolated from different sources. 
PCR determination was repeated. Agarose gel electrophoresis for the PCR determination can be 
seen in Figure 9,10,11,12.  
As shown in Table 7, the presence of six toxin genes (cytK, nheA, CER, hblC, entFM and CES) in 
all isolates were tested by using PCR. None of the strains contained all the six examined genes, but 
almost all strains contained at least two toxin genes except the strain 17544. The emetic toxins CER 
and CES were detected in strains, such as 21792, 10329 and 17533 in both determinations. The en-
terotoxin gene hblC was presents in strains, 21750, 21800, 16238, 11129, 21803, 21791, T101, 
T100, T185, Tomato and 21793. In a previous study, the strain 16238 was investigated for content 
of Bacillus cereus toxin genes and the results was similar with results obtained in this study, with 
the presence of nheA, hblC, and cytK genes (69). The hblC gene was positive in all the mentioned 
strains in both determinations, except for the strain 21800. It may be due to reduced amount of the 
master mix caused by pipetting error. The enterotoxin gene entFM was present almost in all strains, 
except 17544. The nheA gene was also present in all strains except 17544. The cytK gene were neg-
ative for all 20 strains in the first determination, but the second time they were positive for the cytK 
gene for all strains except 17544. This could be again caused by the pipetting errors. For the first 
time, an automatic pipette was used to transfer master mix into the PCR strips, which reduced the 
volume, or the reason could also be that no primer was added.  
The strain 21750 isolated from an unusual strain (NVH 391-98), which was responsible for a se-
vere, food-borne outbreak of diarrheal disease in France in 1998 after consumption of vegetable pu-
ree, where three of them died. The isolated strain contained the cytK gene, which was also possible 
to test in the present study (14).  
According to the study Rosenquist et al. 2006 (69) the strain 17544 contained the toxin genes, con-
sist of nheA, hblC, CER and cry3 genes, but in this study, none of the emetic or enterotoxin genes 
were present in the strain 17544. Whereas, it was possible to identify the strain 17544 by MALDI-
TOF MS, which has shown the content of two Bacillus spp., such as B. weihenstephanensis and B. 
mycoides. Results for the first and second PCR determinations is illustrated more detailed in Appen-
dix 8a: PCR- Gel electrophoresis for first determination and Appendix 8b: PCR- Gel electrophore-
sis for second determination.

45	    
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
Growth	  experiments	  	  
 
This section describes the growth experiments for the 20 different strains produced of Bacillus ce-
reus sensu lato applied in this study. Growth on solid BA versus liquid BHI-broth was investigated 
at a temperature range from 45 to 52 °C. At temperatures where strains did not show growth on 
BA-plates, growth experiments were repeated in BHI-broth, as according to the study Dana M. et 
al. 2000 (80) describes that Bacillus cereus grows best in liquid foods.  
In a previous study, the response of Bacillus cereus growth in different agar concentrations was in-
vestigated, by increasing viscosity of the liquid phase of the agar media. The viscosities of BHI so-
lutions contained different volumes (100, 150, 200, 250 and 300 g/l) of polyvinylpyrrolidone 
 (PVP). Bacillus cereus growth were determined at 30 °C in 24 h incubation. It showed, when the 
agar concentration was increased, the colony areas were reduced significantly (81).  
Gould et al. (1988) examined, that high viscosity in food can inhibit the growth of microorganisms, 
because of the transport processes between cells and the aqueous environment will slow down (82).  
 
Growth	  on	  BA-­‐plates	  	  
In order to investigate the growth of the different Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains, growth on BA-
plates was first investigated. All 20 strains were first streak-plated onto BA and incubated at differ-
ent temperature of 30, 37, 40 and 42 °C, as the growth temperature range for Bacillus cereus sensu 
lato is 5 to 50 °C, with an optimum around 28-35 °C (83). As expected, growth was observed on all 
BA- plates after one day of incubation at 30, 37, 40 and 42 °C. Subsequently, all the 20 strains that 
showed growth up to 42 °C were tested further for growth on BA-plates at higher temperatures up 
to 50 °C, as it is illustrated in Table 14. Strains, such as 16238, 21803, and C261, did not grow at 45 
°C, and thereafter they were not investigated for higher temperatures, which are noted as n.i. (not 
investigated) in Table 14. The next temperature examined was at 48 °C, and strains, as 11129, 
T101, T100, 17544, T183 and T185 did not showed growth on BA. At 49 °C, growth was seen for 
the strains, as 21750, 21792, 21800, 10329, and 17533. The highest examined temperature for these 
strains was 50 °C, but none of the strains showed growth on BA-plates.  
==PAGE:47==
DTU	  National	  Food	  Institute	  Research	  Group	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  s093510	  Mehtap	  Polat	  Akyol	  	  
for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
46	    
Afterwards it was chosen to decrease the incubation temperature. All the 20 strains were incubated 
at 12 °C as they showed growth at 30 °C, and all 20 strains grew on BA-plates after 3 days incuba-
tion at 12 °C. At 10 °C with an incubation for 3 days, growth was seen of these strains, 16238, 
21803, and T126.  
The next investigated low temperature for Bacillus cereus strains was at 8 °C, and only three of the 
strains showed growth on BA-plates after 3 days of incubation, which were 16238, 21803 and 
T126. Then, these 3 strains were incubated at 5 °C in 3 days, and none of the strains showed 
growth. Some Bacillus cereus sensu lato group strains can grow at 4 °C (9), but in the present study 
no strains were observed growing at temperatures lower than 8 C°.

peratures.	  Temperature	  that	  was	  not	  studied	  is	  marked	  as	  n.i.	  which	  means	  not	  investigated.

47	    
Selection of the strains  
 
Strains to the challenge tests were tested by investigating growth ability in BHI-broth and on BA-
plates.  
Growth	  in	  BHI-­‐broth	  	  	  
To investigate growth in liquid substrate, the 20 Bacillus cereus strains were examined in BHI-
broth for different temperatures at 30-55 °C, as shown in Table 15. The BHI-broth was seeded with 
the examined strains in a 15 ml glass tube. With the exception of strain 21793, all other showed 
growth at temperature 30, 37 and 42 °C in the BHI-broth. At 45 °C, no growth was seen of these 
strains, 16238, 21803, C261 and 21793. Strains showed growth at 45 °C were incubated at 48 °C 
and strains consist of 11129, T101, T100, 17544, T185 and Tomato showed growth at 48 °C. Incu-
bation at 49, 50 and 51 °C, growth was detected for the strains, as 11129, T101, T100, 17544 and 
T185. The strain 11129 did not showed growth at 52 °C, whereas the other strains (T101, T100, 
17544 and T185) grew at 52 and 53 °C. Finally, the last three strains (T101, T100 and 17544) con-
tinued to grow to 55 °C (Table 15).

perature.	  Origins	  for	  the	  strains	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Table	  14.	  Temperatures	  that	  was	  not	  studied	  is	  marked	  as	  n.i.	  which	  means	  not	  in-­‐
vestigated.

Optical density (OD) measurements were conducted to confirm the growth of strains (17544, 
11129, T101, T185 and T100) at different temperatures between 49 and 55 °C, as the growth of Ba-
cillus cereus in BHI-broth was uncertain, because of possible contamination of the media, as the un-
inoculated controls sometimes were positive. Table 16 illustrates OD-measurement for the selected 
strains at different temperature.

ments	  of	  each	  strain	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  5:	  OD-­‐measurements.

As it is seen from table 16, none of the strains showed growth at 54 °C. Strain 11129 did not show 
growth at any of the used temperatures (49-54 °C). Strain T101 showed growth at 49 and 50 °C, 
whereas the strain 17544 showed growth up to 52 °C. The other strains, such as T100 and T185 
showed growth up to 53 °C (Figure 13).  
These growth limits were different from the results obtained earlier where strains 17544, 11129 and 
T100 had shown growth up to 55 °C and strain T185 grew up to 53 °C.  
The reason for the obtained different results with the two different kinds of growth experiment in 
BHI-broth may be due to the BHI-broth, used in the first experiment, being stored at room tempera-
ture in between use and becoming contaminated by opening several times or being stored for too 
long. Growth in the BHI-broth in the first experiment may, therefore, be due to growth of other bac-
teria, than Bacillus, since growth in the medium was not identified, and it is unknown which bacte-
ria that grew. The OD-measurements method was more correct as growth was first examined in wa-
ter bath and later on BA-plates to confirm that growth in BHI-broth in water-bath actually was from 
Bacillus.

DTU	  National	  Food	  Institute	  Research	  Group	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  s093510	  Mehtap	  Polat	  Akyol	  	  
for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
50	    
Bacillus cereus sensu lato comprises seven phylogenetic groups (I, II, III, IV, V, VI, VII) (15), but 
in this study, only five phylogenetic groups were identified, such as I, II, III, IV, and VI, whereas 
the other two groups VI and VII were not found. Group VI is most common in dairy products, such 
as raw milk, chilled pasteurized milk/vegetables, milk products (cheese, cream, ice cream etc.) (15).  
Several studies have shown, that the group VII is existing in dried or dehydrated products, such as 
paprika, sesame, powdered infant formula, contaminated mashed potatoes produced from dehy-
drated product, etc. (84) (85) (86) (87). This may be the reason, why these two groups (VI and VII) 
were not detected in the collected group of strains, as they belonged to rice, pasta, tomato, and so 
on.  
After performing growth experiments on BA-plates, it was assumed that the strains 21793 and C261 
belonged to the phylogenetic group I (B. pseudomycoides, mesophilic), that can grow at tempera-
tures between 10-43 °C. The strain C261 tested grew between 10-42 °C, but strain 21793 grew only 
at 30, 37 and 42 °C. These two strains showed the same growth ability in BHI-broth.  
The strain C261 grew at temperatures between 10-42 °C and 21793 grew at temperatures between 
30-45 °C. MALDI-TOF MS identification has shown (Table 10) that the strain C261 contained 
three Bacillus spp. (B. weihenstephanensis, B. cereus, and B. mycoides). One of them was B. my-
coides which is found both in group IV (Mesophilic) and VI (Psychrotolerant) that have growth 
limits between 10-45 °C and 5-37 °C, respectively. If it is assumed that MALDI-TOF has a correct 
identification of the strain C261, the strain will be located in group IV instead of group I.  
Results for the strain 21793 was very uncertain as it constantly provided varying results. The 
growth experiment on BA-plates were repeated several times to obtain significant growth on the 
plate. It is believed that, the strain went into a dormant spore form, during sub-culturing, and there-
fore, became unable to grow on BA-plates without some kind of activation.  
The strain T126 was placed into group II as it had growth temperature between 8-49 °C (Psychrot-
olerant). Seven out of 20 strains (21792, 21800, 21750, 10329, 17533, C230, and T147) were lo-
cated in group III which has a mesophilic characteristic and consist of B. cereus, B. thuringiensis 
and B. anthracis. The other eight strains as, 11129, 21791, T101, T100, 17544, T185, T183 and To-
mato were located in group IV, due to the growth temperature between 10-48 °C (15).  
 
 
==PAGE:52==
DTU	  National	  Food	  Institute	  Research	  Group	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  s093510	  Mehtap	  Polat	  Akyol	  	  
for	  Microbiology	  and	  Hygiene	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Master	  Thesis	  in	  Food	  Technology	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	      
	  
	  
	  
51	    
And the last two strains, such as 16238 and 21803 were placed in group V, because of their ability 
to grow between 8-42 °C. The phylogenetic group V (mesophilic), contains these bacteria, like, B. 
cereus, B. thuringiensis and B. toyonensis. The MALDI-TOF MS identification showed also pres-
ence of B. cereus and B. thuringiensis for the strain 16238.  
An assumption table was made of the Bacillus cereus sensu lato phylogenetic groups, based on the 
growth of Bacillus cereus strains on BA-plates. When comparing the assumed groups, it gave fairly 
similar answers to other studies (15), e.g. strains, such as 17533, 21792 and 10329 were localized to 
the phylogenetic group III. By subsequent identification with MALDI-TOF MS, it was possible to 
see which Bacillus spp. the applied strains belonged to, that gave several results for some strains 
(16238, 17544 and C261). Subsequently, the PCR-method used to investigate, which genes were 
contained tin he applied strains contributed to a clearer result. In a previously study it was deter-
mined that, each phylogenetic group contained the nhe gene. Group III contained only the emetic 
toxins, CES and CER. The hblC gene were found both in groups I, II, IV, V and VI. B. cytotoxicus 
which is located in group VII, contained the cytK gene. The cytK gene was also present in group I 
strains (88).  
After the PCR method it was detected that the strains 17533, 21792 and 10329 contained the CES 
and CER genes, which corresponds to the assumed phylogenetic group, since group III contained 
the emetic genes, CES and CER. E.g. none of the applied isolates contained B. cytotoxicus, which is 
located in group VII (88) relative to the MALDI-TOF MS identification, which is in agreement 
with the assumed phylogenetic groups.

52	    
Spore	  production	  	  
 
After the two experiments performed to find out growth potential on blood agar and in liquid sub-
strate (BHI) at different temperature (5-55 °C), three strains, such as C230, T100 and T185 were 
selected for the challenge tests. These selected test strains were appropriate for the challenge tests, 
as they were able to grow in a liquid substrate at temperatures ranging from 49 to 53 °C. Spores 
were produced by following the method written in section materials and methods.   
Concentrations (CFU/ml) of the produced spores were calculated to approx. 8 log(CFU/ml) for all 
three strains which is shown in appendix 2.

53	    
Challenge tests  
Microbiological challenge testing was carried out to test growth conditions in purchased food (as-
paragus soup and Mornay sauce) with the previously selected Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains in 
section growth experiment. It has been done initially by producing spores (C230, T100 and T185) 
and then making a pre-test in BHI-broth with the produced spores. Hereafter, the produced spores 
and the method performed in pre-tests in BHI were used for the main tests in two liquid foods. Fi-
nally, the achieved results from the performed experiments are interpreted using statistical analyses.  
 
Pre-­‐tests	  in	  BHI-­‐broth	  	  	  
 
Hot-holding pre-tests was completed in BHI-broth at different temperatures in the range from 45 to 
52 °C with the produced spores of strains C230, T100 and T185, in order to investigate, how the 
chosen Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains growth ability was in BHI-broth within 6 h. The desired 
initial concentration of the spores was 104 per ml to be able to count bacteria grown on TSA-plates. 
Detected overgrowth on TSA-plates was estimated by assuming, that 150 colonies were counted on 
the plate. The following section presents results for heating at 45 °C. Results for the other tempera-
tures, 48, 50 and 52 °C, are illustrated in Appendix 9a, 9b and 9c: Pre-tests in BHI broth.  
Table 18 indicates average concentrations (n=2) of the chosen strains with the different sampling 
times.

54	    
The strain C230 had a start concentration of 4.33 log(CFU/ml) before heating which was reduced to 
4.22 log(CFU/ml) after heating at 70 ℃ for 20 min. A reduction in the same level observed for 
T185, whereas the concentration of T100 decreased 0.7 log(CFU/ml) as a result of heat treatment, 
indicating death of vegetative cells.  
It is seen, that C230 grew with approx. 1 log unit from 4.22 log(CFU/ml) to 5.12 log(CFU/ml) 
within 3 h of incubation at 45℃, as the spores germinated into vegetative cells and multiplied due 
to good nutrition in the BHI-broth. The other strains, T100 and T185, grew first after 6 h incubation 
at 45℃. After 6 h, the agar plates used for counting were overgrown for all strains, C230, T100 and 
T185. The concentration at 6 h in all strains was, therefore, estimated to be 6.70 log(CFU/ml), due 
to overgrowth.

55	    
Main	  tests	  in	  foods	  	  
 
The following section will describe results from the main tests in food. In order to examine how the 
chosen strains can grow in liquid food, main tests have been conducted with purchased asparagus 
soup and Mornay sauce. Duplicates were made of each strain (C230, T100 and T185) with the dif-
ferent food. Table 19 illustrates results for the asparagus soup, which were performed within 6 
hours incubation at 45 °C. Main tests executed with Mornay sauce is illustrated in Table 20. The 
same method has been used for the Mornay sauce as asparagus soup. The main tests have been re-
peated with different temperature, such as 45, 48, 50 and 52 °C. Results for the other temperatures 
(48, 50 and 52 °C) for asparagus soup showed in Appendix 10a, 10b, and 10c: Main tests in Aspar-
agus soup and Mornay sauce is showed in Appendix 11a, 11b and 11c, Main tests in Mornay sauce.

The initial concentration of the strains C230, T100 and T185 in the asparagus soup was calculated 
as it is seen in Table 19. None of the strains showed growth within 3 h incubation at 45 °C, but after 
6 h incubation all three strains grew. The strain C230 grew with approx. 2 log-unit from 4.3 
log(CFU/ml) to 6.1 log(CFU/ml). The other strain T100 grew with approx. 1 log unit-from 4.0 
log(CFU/ml) to 4.9 and the strain T185 grew also with approx. 1 log-unit from 4.80 log(CFU/ml) to 
5.9 log(CFU/ml) due to germination of spores.  
As seen in Table 19 the average concentration (n=2) of the Bacillus cereus strains, C230, T100 and 
T185 shows, the highest concentrations of Bacillus cereus were achieved after 6 h of incubation at 
45 ℃, as the bacteria have good opportunity to grow due to the nutrients contained in the asparagus 
soup. The dominant strain is C230 with a concentration at 6.1 log(CFU/ml) and SD of 0.05.

To analyze Bacillus cereus sensu lato growth in Mornay sauce, the same method was used as for 
the asparagus soup. The strains C230 and T185 grew with approx. 2 log-unit within 6 h incubation 
at 45 °C. Whereas, the strain T100 did not grow as it had an initial concentration of 4.5 
log(CFU/ml) and final concentration of 4.6 log(CFU/ml) (Table 20).   
As it is seen from Table 20, there was a high variation between repeated tests with different strains 
in Mornay sauce. The strain T185 showed the highest standard deviation between sampling T0, 3 h 
and 6 h, such as 0.60, 0.70 and 0.10 log(CFU/ml).  
The reason for the variances between determinations could be, because of the Mornay sauce was a 
bit viscous, and it made the sampling difficult. To solve this pipetting problem, the volume of the 
sauce was increased to 10 ml (1000 µl Mornay sauce + 900 ml 0.9 % NaCl), but still it was not 
mixed well.

growth of Bacillus cereus strains (Figure 14). pH for the asparagus soup was measured to 6,0 and 
for the Mornay sauce was measured to 6.5. pH measurements will be discussed in section, Summa-
rizing of the growth experiment.

58	    
Summarizing of the growth experiments 
 
After conducting pre-tests in BHI and main tests in foods, consisting of asparagus soup and Mornay 
sauce, a summarizing of growth experiment of the Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains, growth was 
defined as differences between T6-T0 or/and T6-T3 as log increase of 0.5 by incubation at different 
temperatures, 45, 48, 50, and 52 °C (Table 21). The used different strains were isolated from white 
rice (T100), organic brown rice (T185) and Mac n’ cheese pasta dish (C230).  
Counting of the TSA-plates provided uncertainty, as growth on some of the plates were based on 
counts of quite a few colonies, e.g. 0 colony of the 10-1 dilution factor and 1 colony of the 10-2 dilu-
tion factor, which was very uncertain. The few colonies on the TSA-plate cause too much uncer-
tainty (89).  
Results from the pre-tests in BHI and main tests in foods (asparagus soup, and Mornay sauce) ex-
periments were used to test for statistical differences between strains, and substrates for each tem-
perature used to confirm the growth of Bacillus cereus sensu lato. For assessment of growth differ-
ences, two-way ANOVA with replication for T0 were performed with substrates and strains for each 
temperature (45, 48, 50 and 52 °C). ANOVA calculations were carried out on Microsoft Office Ex-
cel. Differences with p < 0.05 were considered significant.  
 
Table 21, illustrates summarizing of Bacillus cereus sensu lato strains (C230, T100 and T185) 
growth ability, at different incubation temperatures within six hours in different substrates (BHI-
broth, asparagus soup and Mornay sauce).

59	    
 
 
As seen from Table 21 all the applied strains as C230, T100 and T185 grew at 45 °C in BHI-broth, 
asparagus soup and in Mornay sauce, whereas at 48 °C strains T100 and T185 grew in BHI-broth 
and in Mornay sauce (Table 21). Duplicates were made of all samples and in Mornay sauce, the 
strain T100 growth was seen at 45 and 48 °C only in one of the determinations, and at 50 °C growth 
were obtained in both of the determinations. The strain T100 showed growth at 52 °C in BHI-broth 
in one of the determinations. It was assumed (Table 17) that the strain T100 belonged to the phylo-
genetic group IV with a growth temperature between 12-45 °C. Various studies (14) (15) (17) have 
shown, that the phylogenetic group IV can grow with pH conditions between 4.6-7.4 (Table 1) and 
with salt content between 0.5-8 % (Table 2). The pH and salt content in Mornay sauce were, 
pH=6.5, and 1.75 % and in asparagus soup were pH= 6.0 and salt content=0.7%, which fits well 
with the phylogenetic group IV’s growth conditions and agrees with the other studies.  
The strain C230 grew at 45 °C in all substrates, such as BHI-broth, Asparagus soup and Mornay 
sauce and at 48 °C did not grew. Then, at 50 °C growth was seen in BHI-broth and in Mornay sauce 
in only one of the determinations.
